<p>A <b>bicycle</b>, also called a <b>pedal cycle</b>, <b>bike</b> or <b>cycle</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Human-powered_transport" title="Human-powered transport">human-powered</a> or <a href="/wiki/Motorized_bicycle" title="Motorized bicycle">motor-powered</a> assisted, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_pedal" title="Bicycle pedal">pedal-driven</a>, <a href="/wiki/Single-track_vehicle" title="Single-track vehicle">single-track vehicle</a>, having two <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_wheel" title="Bicycle wheel">wheels</a> attached to a <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_frame" title="Bicycle frame">frame</a>, one behind the other. A <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023754711">.mw-parser-output .vanchor>:target~.vanchor-text{background-color:#b1d2ff}</style><span class="vanchor"><span id="bicycle_rider"></span><span id="BICYCLE_RIDER"></span><span class="vanchor-text">bicycle rider</span></span> is called a cyclist, or bicyclist.
</p><p>Bicycles were introduced in the 19th century in Europe. By the early 21st century, more than 1 billion were in existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koeppel2007_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Koeppel2007-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economist2011_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Economist2011-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldmeters_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-worldmeters-3">[3]</a></sup> These numbers far exceed the number of cars, both in total and ranked by the number of individual models produced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squatriglia2008_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Squatriglia2008-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMA2006_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMA2006-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-6">[6]</a></sup> They are the principal <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Means_of_transportation" title="Means of transportation">means of transportation</a> in many regions. They also provide a popular form of <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreation</a>, and have been adapted for use as children's toys, <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">general fitness</a>, military and police applications, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Courier_services" title="Courier services">courier services</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicycle_racing" title="Bicycle racing">bicycle racing</a>, and bicycle stunts.
</p><p>The basic shape and configuration of a typical <a href="/wiki/Safety_bicycle" title="Safety bicycle">upright or "safety bicycle"</a>, has changed little since the first <a href="/wiki/Chain_drive" title="Chain drive">chain-driven</a> model was developed around 1885.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004200–50_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004200–50-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004266–71_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004266–71-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004280_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004280-9">[9]</a></sup> However, many details have been improved, especially since the advent of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_materials" title="Modern materials">modern materials</a> and <a href="/wiki/Computer-aided_design" title="Computer-aided design">computer-aided design</a>. These have allowed for a proliferation of specialized designs for many types of cycling.
</p><p>The bicycle's invention has had an enormous effect on society, both in terms of culture and of advancing modern industrial methods. Several components that played a key role in the development of the automobile were initially invented for use in the bicycle, including <a href="/wiki/Ball_bearing" title="Ball bearing">ball bearings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tire" title="Tire">pneumatic tires</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chain-driven_sprocket" title="Chain-driven sprocket">chain-driven sprockets</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wire_wheels" title="Wire wheels">tension-spoked wheels</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although bike and cycle are used interchangeably to refer mostly to two types of two-wheelers, the terms still vary across the world. In India, for example, a cycle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> refers only to a two-wheeler using pedal power whereas the term bike is used to describe a two-wheeler using <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engine</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_motors" title="Electric motors">electric motors</a> as a source of motive power instead of motorcycle/motorbike.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The word <i>bicycle</i> first appeared in English print in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_News_(UK)" title="The Daily News (UK)">The Daily News</a></i> in 1868, to describe "Bysicles and trysicles" on the "Champs Elysées and Bois de Boulogne".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-OED-12">[12]</a></sup> The word was first used in 1847 in a French publication to describe an unidentified two-wheeled vehicle, possibly a carriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-OED-12">[12]</a></sup> The design of the bicycle was an advance on the <a href="/wiki/Velocipede" title="Velocipede">velocipede</a>, although the words were used with some degree of overlap for a time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OED_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-OED-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other words for bicycle include "bike",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> "pushbike",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> "pedal cycle",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> or "cycle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Unicode" title="Unicode">Unicode</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Code_point" title="Code point">code point</a> for "bicycle" is 0x1F6B2. The <a href="/wiki/Numeric_character_reference" title="Numeric character reference">entity</a> <code>&amp;#x1F6B2;</code> in <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a> produces 🚲.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_bicycle" title="History of the bicycle">History of the bicycle</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bicycle&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The "<a href="/wiki/Dandy_horse" title="Dandy horse">dandy horse</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> also called <i>Draisienne</i> or <i>Laufmaschine</i> ("running machine"), was the first human means of transport to use only two wheels in <a href="/wiki/Tandem" title="Tandem">tandem</a> and was invented by the German <a href="/wiki/Freiherr" title="Freiherr">Baron</a> <a href="/wiki/Karl_Drais" title="Karl Drais">Karl von Drais</a>. It is regarded as the first bicycle, but it did not have pedals;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Drais introduced it to the public in <a href="/wiki/Mannheim" title="Mannheim">Mannheim</a> in 1817 and in Paris in 1818.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csts_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-csts-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy200426_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy200426-25">[25]</a></sup> Its rider sat astride a wooden frame supported by two in-line wheels and pushed the vehicle along with his or her feet while steering the front wheel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csts_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-csts-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first mechanically propelled, two-wheeled vehicle may have been built by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kirkpatrick_MacMillan" title="Kirkpatrick MacMillan">Kirkpatrick MacMillan</a>, a Scottish blacksmith, in 1839, although the claim is often disputed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy200466–67_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy200466–67-26">[26]</a></sup> He is also associated with the first recorded instance of a cycling traffic offense, when a Glasgow newspaper in 1842 reported an accident in which an anonymous "gentleman from Dumfries-shire... bestride a velocipede... of ingenious design" knocked over a little girl in Glasgow and was fined five <a href="/wiki/Shilling" title="Shilling">shillings</a> (equivalent to £25 in 2021).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the early 1860s, Frenchmen <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Michaux" title="Pierre Michaux">Pierre Michaux</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Lallement" title="Pierre Lallement">Pierre Lallement</a> took bicycle design in a new direction by adding a mechanical <a href="/wiki/Crank_(mechanism)" title="Crank (mechanism)">crank</a> drive with pedals on an enlarged front wheel (the <a href="/wiki/Velocipede" title="Velocipede">velocipede</a>). This was the first in mass production. Another French inventor named Douglas Grasso had a failed prototype of Pierre Lallement's bicycle several years earlier. Several inventions followed using rear-wheel drive, the best known being the rod-driven velocipede by Scotsman <a href="/wiki/Thomas_McCall_(inventor)" title="Thomas McCall (inventor)">Thomas McCall</a> in 1869. In that same year, bicycle wheels with wire spokes were patented by <a href="/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Meyer_(inventor)" title="Eugène Meyer (inventor)">Eugène Meyer</a> of Paris.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The French <i>vélocipède</i>, made of iron and wood, developed into the "<a href="/wiki/Penny-farthing" title="Penny-farthing">penny-farthing</a>" (historically known as an "ordinary bicycle", a <a href="/wiki/Retronym" title="Retronym">retronym</a>, since there was then no other kind).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200150_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200150-29">[29]</a></sup> It featured a tubular steel frame on which were mounted wire-spoked wheels with solid rubber tires. These bicycles were difficult to ride due to their high seat and poor <a href="/wiki/Weight_distribution" title="Weight distribution">weight distribution</a>. In 1868 Rowley Turner, a sales agent of the Coventry Sewing Machine Company (which soon became the <a href="/wiki/Hillman#History" title="Hillman">Coventry Machinists Company</a>), brought a Michaux cycle to <a href="/wiki/Coventry" title="Coventry">Coventry</a>, England. His uncle, Josiah Turner, and business partner <a href="/wiki/James_Starley" title="James Starley">James Starley</a>, used this as a basis for the 'Coventry Model' in what became Britain's first cycle factory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>dwarf ordinary</i> addressed some of these faults by reducing the front wheel <a href="/wiki/Diameter" title="Diameter">diameter</a> and setting the <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_seat" title="Bicycle seat">seat</a> further back. This, in turn, required gearing—effected in a variety of ways—to efficiently use pedal power. Having to both pedal and steer via the front wheel remained a problem. Englishman <a href="/wiki/John_Kemp_Starley" title="John Kemp Starley">J.K. Starley</a> (nephew of James Starley), J.H. Lawson, and Shergold solved this problem by introducing the <a href="/wiki/Chain_drive" title="Chain drive">chain drive</a> (originated by the unsuccessful "bicyclette" of Englishman Henry Lawson),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200147_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200147-31">[31]</a></sup> connecting the frame-mounted cranks to the rear wheel. These models were known as <a href="/wiki/Safety_bicycle" title="Safety bicycle">safety bicycles</a>, dwarf safeties, or upright bicycles for their lower seat height and better weight distribution, although without pneumatic tires the ride of the smaller-wheeled bicycle would be much rougher than that of the larger-wheeled variety. Starley's 1885 <a href="/wiki/Rover_Company" title="Rover Company">Rover</a>, manufactured in Coventry<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> is usually described as the first recognizably modern bicycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Soon the <i>seat tube</i> was added which created the modern bike's double-triangle <i>diamond frame</i>.
</p><p>Further innovations increased comfort and ushered in a second <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicycle_craze" title="Bicycle craze">bicycle craze</a>, the 1890s <i>Golden Age of Bicycles</i>. In 1888, Scotsman <a href="/wiki/John_Boyd_Dunlop" title="John Boyd Dunlop">John Boyd Dunlop</a> introduced the first practical pneumatic tire, which soon became universal. <a href="/wiki/Willie_Hume" title="Willie Hume">Willie Hume</a> demonstrated the supremacy of Dunlop's tyres in 1889, winning the tyre's first-ever races in Ireland and then England.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gold_Hume_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gold_Hume-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunlop_time_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunlop_time-35">[35]</a></sup> Soon after, the rear <a href="/wiki/Freewheel" title="Freewheel">freewheel</a> was developed, enabling the rider to coast. This refinement led to the 1890s invention<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coaster_brake" title="Coaster brake">coaster brakes</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Derailleur_gears" title="Derailleur gears">Dérailleur gears</a> and hand-operated <a href="/wiki/Bowden_cable" title="Bowden cable">Bowden cable</a>-pull brakes were also developed during these years, but were only slowly adopted by casual riders.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Svea_Velocipede" title="Svea Velocipede">Svea Velocipede</a> with vertical pedal arrangement and <a href="/wiki/Locking_hubs" title="Locking hubs">locking hubs</a> was introduced in 1892 by the Swedish engineers <a href="/wiki/Fredrik_Ljungstr%C3%B6m" title="Fredrik Ljungström">Fredrik Ljungström</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birger_Ljungstr%C3%B6m" title="Birger Ljungström">Birger Ljungström</a>. It attracted attention at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Fair" title="World Fair">World Fair</a> and was produced in a few thousand units.
</p><p>In the 1870s many <a href="/wiki/Cycling_club" title="Cycling club">cycling clubs</a> flourished. They were popular in a time when there were not cars on the market and the principal mode of transportation was <a href="/wiki/Horse-drawn_vehicle" title="Horse-drawn vehicle">horse-drawn vehicles</a>, such the <a href="/wiki/Horse_and_buggy" title="Horse and buggy">horse and buggy</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Horsecar" title="Horsecar">horsecar</a>. Among the earliest clubs was <a href="/wiki/Cycling_UK" title="Cycling UK">The Bicycle Touring Club</a>, which has operated since 1878. By the turn of the century, cycling clubs flourished on both sides of the Atlantic, and touring and racing became widely popular. The <a href="/wiki/Raleigh_Bicycle_Company" title="Raleigh Bicycle Company">Raleigh Bicycle Company</a> was founded in Nottingham, England in 1888. It became the biggest bicycle manufacturing company in the world, making over two million bikes per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bicycles and horse buggies were the two mainstays of private transportation just prior to the automobile, and the grading of smooth roads in the late 19th century was stimulated by the widespread advertising, production, and use of these devices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004280_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004280-9">[9]</a></sup> More than 1 billion bicycles have been manufactured worldwide as of the early 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koeppel2007_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Koeppel2007-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economist2011_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Economist2011-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-worldmeters_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-worldmeters-3">[3]</a></sup> Bicycles are the most common vehicle of any kind in the world, and the most numerous model of any kind of vehicle, whether human-powered or <a href="/wiki/Motor_vehicle" title="Motor vehicle">motor vehicle</a>, is the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Flying_Pigeon" title="Flying Pigeon">Flying Pigeon</a>, with numbers exceeding 500 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koeppel2007_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Koeppel2007-1">[1]</a></sup> The next most numerous vehicle, the <a href="/wiki/Honda_Super_Cub" title="Honda Super Cub">Honda Super Cub</a> motorcycle, has more than 100 million units made,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SuperCub_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-SuperCub-38">[38]</a></sup> while most produced car, the <a href="/wiki/Toyota_Corolla" title="Toyota Corolla">Toyota Corolla</a>, has reached 44 million and counting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Squatriglia2008_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Squatriglia2008-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMA2006_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMA2006-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ABC_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ABC-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FoxBusiness_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FoxBusiness-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>

The first modern bicycle, it featured a rear-wheel-drive, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chain-drive" title="Chain-drive">chain-driven</a> cycle with two similar-sized wheels. Dunlop's <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_tire" title="Bicycle tire">pneumatic tire</a> was added to the bicycle in 1888.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bicycle&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>From the beginning, bicycles have been and continue to be employed for many uses. In a utilitarian way, bicycles are used for transportation, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_commuting" title="Bicycle commuting">bicycle commuting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Utility_cycling" title="Utility cycling">utility cycling</a>. It can be used as a 'work horse' by <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_mail" title="Bicycle mail">mail carriers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paramedic" title="Paramedic">paramedics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Police_bicycle" title="Police bicycle">police</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_messenger" title="Bicycle messenger">messengers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cargo_bike" title="Cargo bike">general delivery</a> services. Military uses of bicycles include <a href="/wiki/Military_communications" title="Military communications">communications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reconnaissance" title="Reconnaissance">reconnaissance</a>, troop movement, supply of provisions, and patrol. See also: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_infantry" title="Bicycle infantry">bicycle infantry</a>.
</p><p>The bicycle is also used for recreational purposes, such as <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_touring" title="Bicycle touring">bicycle touring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mountain_biking" title="Mountain biking">mountain biking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_fitness" title="Physical fitness">physical fitness</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Play_(activity)" title="Play (activity)">play</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cycle_sport" title="Cycle sport">Bicycle competition</a> includes <a href="/wiki/Road_bicycle_racing" title="Road bicycle racing">racing</a>, <a href="/wiki/BMX_racing" title="BMX racing">BMX racing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Track_cycling" title="Track cycling">track racing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Criterium" title="Criterium">criterium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_rollers" title="Bicycle rollers">roller racing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyclosportive" title="Cyclosportive">sportives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time_trial" title="Time trial">time trials</a>. Major multi-stage professional events are the <a href="/wiki/Giro_d%27Italia" title="Giro d'Italia">Giro d'Italia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_France" title="Tour de France">Tour de France</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vuelta_a_Espa%C3%B1a" title="Vuelta a España">Vuelta a España</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tour_de_Pologne" title="Tour de Pologne">Tour de Pologne</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Volta_a_Portugal" title="Volta a Portugal">Volta a Portugal</a>.
</p><p>Bikes can be used for entertainment and pleasure, such as in organised mass rides, <a href="/wiki/Artistic_cycling" title="Artistic cycling">artistic cycling</a> and <a href="/wiki/Freestyle_BMX" title="Freestyle BMX">freestyle BMX</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_aspects">Technical aspects</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bicycle&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Firefighter_bicycle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3240" data-file-width="4320" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Firefighter_bicycle.jpg/220px-Firefighter_bicycle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Firefighter_bicycle.jpg/330px-Firefighter_bicycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Firefighter_bicycle.jpg/440px-Firefighter_bicycle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Firefighter_bicycle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Firefighter bicycle</div></div></div>
<p>The bicycle has undergone continual adaptation and improvement since its inception. These innovations have continued with the advent of modern materials and computer-aided design, allowing for a proliferation of specialized bicycle types, improved <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_safety" title="Bicycle safety">bicycle safety</a>, and riding comfort.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_bicycle_types" title="List of bicycle types">List of bicycle types</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_man_riding_an_electric_bicycle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1494" data-file-width="2656" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/A_man_riding_an_electric_bicycle.jpg/220px-A_man_riding_an_electric_bicycle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/A_man_riding_an_electric_bicycle.jpg/330px-A_man_riding_an_electric_bicycle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/A_man_riding_an_electric_bicycle.jpg/440px-A_man_riding_an_electric_bicycle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_man_riding_an_electric_bicycle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A man riding an <a href="/wiki/Electric_bicycle" title="Electric bicycle">electric bicycle</a></div></div></div>
<p>Bicycles can be categorized in many different ways: by function, by number of riders, by general construction, by gearing or by means of propulsion. The more common types include <a href="/wiki/Utility_bicycle" title="Utility bicycle">utility bicycles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mountain_bicycle" title="Mountain bicycle">mountain bicycles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Racing_bicycle" title="Racing bicycle">racing bicycles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Touring_bicycle" title="Touring bicycle">touring bicycles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_bicycle" title="Hybrid bicycle">hybrid bicycles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cruiser_bicycle" title="Cruiser bicycle">cruiser bicycles</a>, and <a href="/wiki/BMX_bike" title="BMX bike">BMX bikes</a>. Less common are <a href="/wiki/Tandem_bicycle" title="Tandem bicycle">tandems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lowrider_bicycle" title="Lowrider bicycle">low riders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tall_bike" title="Tall bike">tall bikes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fixed-gear_bicycle" title="Fixed-gear bicycle">fixed gear</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folding_bicycle" title="Folding bicycle">folding models</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphibious_bicycles" title="Amphibious bicycles">amphibious bicycles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cargo_bike" title="Cargo bike">cargo bikes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recumbent_bicycle" title="Recumbent bicycle">recumbents</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_bicycles" title="Electric bicycles">electric bicycles</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Unicycle" title="Unicycle">Unicycles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tricycle" title="Tricycle">tricycles</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quadracycle_(human-powered_vehicle)" title="Quadracycle (human-powered vehicle)">quadracycles</a> are not strictly bicycles, as they have respectively one, three and four wheels, but are often referred to informally as "bikes" or "cycles".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dynamics">Dynamics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_and_motorcycle_dynamics" title="Bicycle and motorcycle dynamics">Bicycle and motorcycle dynamics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jersey_Town_Criterium_2011_81.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2261" data-file-width="1914" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Jersey_Town_Criterium_2011_81.jpg/170px-Jersey_Town_Criterium_2011_81.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Jersey_Town_Criterium_2011_81.jpg/255px-Jersey_Town_Criterium_2011_81.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Jersey_Town_Criterium_2011_81.jpg/340px-Jersey_Town_Criterium_2011_81.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jersey_Town_Criterium_2011_81.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A cyclist leaning in a turn</div></div></div>
<p>A bicycle stays upright while moving forward by being steered so as to keep its <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass" title="Center of mass">center of mass</a> over the wheels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NS2581_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-NS2581-40">[40]</a></sup> This steering is usually provided by the rider, but under certain conditions may be provided by the bicycle itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPRS_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPRS-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>The combined center of mass of a bicycle and its rider must lean into a turn to successfully navigate it. This lean is induced by a method known as <a href="/wiki/Countersteering" title="Countersteering">countersteering</a>, which can be performed by the rider turning the handlebars directly with the hands<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wilson_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wilson-42">[42]</a></sup> or indirectly by leaning the bicycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fajans_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-fajans-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>Short-wheelbase or tall bicycles, when braking, can generate enough stopping force at the front wheel to flip longitudinally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The act of purposefully using this force to lift the rear wheel and balance on the front without tipping over is a trick known as a <a href="/wiki/Stoppie" title="Stoppie">stoppie</a>, endo, or front wheelie.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Performance">Performance</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_performance" title="Bicycle performance">Bicycle performance</a></div>
<p>The bicycle is extraordinarily efficient in both biological and mechanical terms. The bicycle is the most efficient human-powered means of transportation in terms of energy a person must expend to travel a given distance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> From a mechanical viewpoint, up to 99% of the energy delivered by the rider into the pedals is transmitted to the wheels, although the use of gearing mechanisms may reduce this by 10–15%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whitt_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-whitt-47">[47]</a></sup> In terms of the ratio of cargo weight a bicycle can carry to total weight, it is also an efficient means of cargo transportation.
</p><p>A human traveling on a bicycle at low to medium speeds of around 16–24 km/h (10–15 mph) uses only the power required to walk. Air drag, which is proportional to the square of speed, requires dramatically higher power outputs as speeds increase. If the rider is sitting upright, the rider's body creates about 75% of the total drag of the bicycle/rider combination. Drag can be reduced by seating the rider in a more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerodynamic" title="Aerodynamic">aerodynamically</a> streamlined position. Drag can also be reduced by covering the bicycle with an aerodynamic <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_fairing" title="Bicycle fairing">fairing</a>. The fastest recorded unpaced speed on a flat surface is 144.18 km/h (89.59 mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upi_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-upi-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> generated in the production and transportation of the food required by the bicyclist, per mile traveled, is less than <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">10</span></span> that generated by energy efficient motorcars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 269.33333333333px"><div style="width: 269.33333333333px">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Parts">Parts</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bicycle&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frame">Frame</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_frame" title="Bicycle frame">Bicycle frame</a></div>
<p>The great majority of modern bicycles have a frame with upright seating that looks much like the first chain-driven bike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004200–50_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004200–50-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004266–71_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004266–71-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004280_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004280-9">[9]</a></sup> These upright bicycles almost always feature the <i>diamond frame</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Truss" title="Truss">truss</a> consisting of two triangles: the front triangle and the rear triangle. The front triangle consists of the head tube, top tube, down tube, and seat tube. The head tube contains the <a href="/wiki/Headset_(bicycle_part)" title="Headset (bicycle part)">headset</a>, the set of bearings that allows the <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_fork" title="Bicycle fork">fork</a> to turn smoothly for steering and balance. The top tube connects the head tube to the seat tube at the top, and the down tube connects the head tube to the <a href="/wiki/Bottom_bracket" title="Bottom bracket">bottom bracket</a>. The rear triangle consists of the seat tube and paired chain stays and seat stays. The chain stays run parallel to the <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_chain" title="Bicycle chain">chain</a>, connecting the bottom bracket to the rear <a href="/wiki/Fork_end" title="Fork end">dropout</a>, where the axle for the rear wheel is held. The seat stays connect the top of the seat tube (at or near the same point as the top tube) to the rear fork ends.
</p><p>Historically, women's bicycle frames had a top tube that connected in the middle of the seat tube instead of the top, resulting in a lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frame_geometry" title="Frame geometry">standover height</a> at the expense of compromised structural integrity, since this places a strong bending load in the seat tube, and bicycle frame members are typically weak in bending. This design, referred to as a <i><a href="/wiki/Step-through_frame" title="Step-through frame">step-through frame</a></i> or as an <i>open frame</i>, allows the rider to mount and dismount in a dignified way while wearing a skirt or dress. While some women's bicycles continue to use this frame style, there is also a variation, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixte" title="Mixte">mixte</a></i>, which splits the top tube laterally into two thinner top tubes that bypass the seat tube on each side and connect to the rear fork ends. The ease of stepping through is also appreciated by those with limited flexibility or other joint problems. Because of its persistent image as a "women's" bicycle, step-through frames are not common for larger frames.
</p><p>Step-throughs were popular partly for practical reasons and partly for social mores of the day. For most of the history of bicycles' popularity women have worn long skirts, and the lower frame accommodated these better than the top-tube. Furthermore, it was considered "unladylike" for women to open their legs to mount and dismount—in more conservative times women who rode bicycles at all were vilified as immoral or immodest. These practices were akin to the older practice of riding horse <a href="/wiki/Sidesaddle" title="Sidesaddle">sidesaddle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another style is the <a href="/wiki/Recumbent_bicycle" title="Recumbent bicycle">recumbent bicycle</a>. These are inherently more aerodynamic than upright versions, as the rider may lean back onto a support and operate pedals that are on about the same level as the seat. The world's fastest bicycle is a recumbent bicycle but this type was banned from competition in 1934 by the <a href="/wiki/Union_Cycliste_Internationale" title="Union Cycliste Internationale">Union Cycliste Internationale</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Historically, materials used in bicycles have followed a similar pattern as in aircraft, the goal being high strength and low weight. Since the late 1930s alloy steels have been used for frame and fork tubes in higher quality machines. By the 1980s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminum" title="Aluminum">aluminum</a> <a href="/wiki/Welding" title="Welding">welding</a> techniques had improved to the point that aluminum tube could safely be used in place of <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a>. Since then aluminum alloy frames and other components have become popular due to their light weight, and most mid-range bikes are now principally aluminum alloy of some kind.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(geographic_names)" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (geographic names)"><span title="The geographic scope near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2012)">where?</span></a></i>]</sup> More expensive bikes use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fibre" title="Carbon fibre">carbon fibre</a> due to its significantly lighter weight and profiling ability, allowing designers to make a bike both stiff and compliant by manipulating the lay-up. Virtually all professional racing bicycles now use carbon fibre frames, as they have the best strength to weight ratio. A typical modern carbon fiber frame can weighs less than 1 kilogram (2.2 lb).
</p><p>Other exotic frame materials include <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a> and advanced alloys. <a href="/wiki/Bamboo" title="Bamboo">Bamboo</a>, a natural <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite material</a> with high strength-to-weight ratio and <a href="/wiki/Stiffness" title="Stiffness">stiffness</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakkad_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakkad-52">[52]</a></sup> has been used for bicycles since 1894.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lukenbill_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-lukenbill-53">[53]</a></sup> Recent versions use bamboo for the primary frame with glued metal connections and parts, priced as exotic models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lukenbill_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-lukenbill-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drivetrain_and_gearing">Drivetrain and gearing</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_drivetrain_systems" title="Bicycle drivetrain systems">Bicycle drivetrain systems</a></div>
<p>The <i>drivetrain</i> begins with pedals which rotate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crank_set" title="Crank set">cranks</a>, which are held in axis by the bottom bracket. Most bicycles use a chain to transmit power to the rear wheel. A very small number of bicycles use a shaft drive to transmit power, or special belts. Hydraulic bicycle transmissions have been built, but they are currently inefficient and complex.
</p><p>Since cyclists' legs are most efficient over a narrow range of pedaling speeds, or <a href="/wiki/Cadence_(cycling)" title="Cadence (cycling)">cadence</a>, a variable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gear_ratio" title="Gear ratio">gear ratio</a> helps a cyclist to maintain an optimum pedalling speed while covering varied terrain. Some, mainly utility, bicycles use <a href="/wiki/Hub_gear" title="Hub gear">hub gears</a> with between 3 and 14 ratios, but most use the generally more efficient dérailleur system, by which the chain is moved between different cogs called chainrings and sprockets to select a ratio. A dérailleur system normally has two dérailleurs, or mechs, one at the front to select the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chainring" title="Chainring">chainring</a> and another at the back to select the sprocket. Most bikes have two or three chainrings, and from 5 to 11 sprockets on the back, with the number of theoretical gears calculated by multiplying front by back. In reality, many gears overlap or require the chain to run diagonally, so the number of usable gears is fewer.
</p><p>An alternative to chaindrive is to use a synchronous belt. These are toothed and work much the same as a chain—popular with commuters and long distance cyclists they require little maintenance. They can't be shifted across a cassette of sprockets, and are used either as single speed or with a hub gear.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bicycle_gearing" title="Bicycle gearing">Different gears and ranges of gears</a> are appropriate for different people and styles of cycling. Multi-speed bicycles allow gear selection to suit the circumstances: a cyclist could use a high gear when cycling downhill, a medium gear when cycling on a flat road, and a low gear when cycling uphill. In a lower gear every turn of the pedals leads to fewer rotations of the rear wheel. This allows the energy required to move the same distance to be distributed over more pedal turns, reducing fatigue when riding uphill, with a heavy load, or against strong winds. A higher gear allows a cyclist to make fewer pedal turns to maintain a given speed, but with more effort per turn of the pedals.
</p><p>With a <i>chain drive</i> transmission, a <i>chainring</i> attached to a crank drives the chain, which in turn rotates the rear wheel via the rear sprocket(s) (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassette_(bicycle_part)" title="Cassette (bicycle part)">cassette</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cogset#Freewheels" title="Cogset">freewheel</a>). There are four gearing options: two-speed hub gear integrated with chain ring, up to 3 chain rings, up to 11 sprockets, hub gear built into rear wheel (3-speed to 14-speed). The most common options are either a rear hub or multiple chain rings combined with multiple sprockets (other combinations of options are possible but less common).
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
