<div class="gallerytext">
<p>A set of rear sprockets (also known as a cassette) and a <a href="/wiki/Derailleur" title="Derailleur">derailleur</a>
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 215.33333333333px"><div style="width: 215.33333333333px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 213.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hub_gear.jpg" title="Hub gear"><img alt="Hub gear" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hub_gear.jpg/320px-Hub_gear.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hub_gear.jpg/480px-Hub_gear.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Hub_gear.jpg/640px-Hub_gear.jpg 2x" width="214"/></a></div></div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Hub gear
</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steering">Steering</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anatomic_bicycle_grips_made_of_leather.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2944" data-file-width="4568" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Anatomic_bicycle_grips_made_of_leather.jpg/220px-Anatomic_bicycle_grips_made_of_leather.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Anatomic_bicycle_grips_made_of_leather.jpg/330px-Anatomic_bicycle_grips_made_of_leather.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Anatomic_bicycle_grips_made_of_leather.jpg/440px-Anatomic_bicycle_grips_made_of_leather.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anatomic_bicycle_grips_made_of_leather.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bicycle grips made of leather. Anatomic shape distributes weight over <a href="/wiki/Hand" title="Hand">palm area</a> to prevent cyclist's palsy (<a href="/wiki/Ulnar_tunnel_syndrome" title="Ulnar tunnel syndrome">ulnar syndrome</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_handlebar" title="Bicycle handlebar">handlebars</a> connect to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stem_(bike)" title="Stem (bike)">stem</a> that connects to the fork that connects to the front wheel, and the whole assembly connects to the bike and rotates about the steering axis via the <a href="/wiki/Headset_(bicycle_part)" title="Headset (bicycle part)">headset</a> bearings. Three styles of handlebar are common. <i>Upright handlebars</i>, the norm in Europe and elsewhere until the 1970s, curve gently back toward the rider, offering a natural grip and comfortable upright position. <i>Drop handlebars</i> "drop" as they curve forward and down, offering the cyclist best braking power from a more aerodynamic "crouched" position, as well as more upright positions in which the hands grip the brake lever mounts, the forward curves, or the upper flat sections for increasingly upright postures. Mountain bikes generally feature a 'straight handlebar' or 'riser bar' with varying degrees of sweep backwards and centimeters rise upwards, as well as wider widths which can provide better handling due to increased leverage against the wheel.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seating">Seating</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_marco_selle1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/San_marco_selle1.jpg/220px-San_marco_selle1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/San_marco_selle1.jpg/330px-San_marco_selle1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/San_marco_selle1.jpg/440px-San_marco_selle1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_marco_selle1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Selle San Marco saddle designed for women</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bicycle_saddle" title="Bicycle saddle">Saddles</a> also vary with rider preference, from the cushioned ones favored by short-distance riders to narrower saddles which allow more room for leg swings. Comfort depends on riding position. With comfort bikes and hybrids, cyclists sit high over the seat, their weight directed down onto the saddle, such that a wider and more cushioned saddle is preferable. For racing bikes where the rider is bent over, weight is more evenly distributed between the handlebars and saddle, the hips are flexed, and a narrower and harder saddle is more efficient. Differing saddle designs exist for male and female cyclists, accommodating the genders' differing anatomies and sit bone width measurements, although bikes typically are sold with saddles most appropriate for men. Suspension seat posts and seat springs provide comfort by absorbing shock but can add to the overall weight of the bicycle.
</p><p>A recumbent bicycle has a reclined <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_seat" title="Bicycle seat">chair-like seat</a> that some riders find more comfortable than a saddle, especially riders who suffer from certain types of seat, back, neck, shoulder, or wrist pain. Recumbent bicycles may have either under-seat or over-seat <a href="/wiki/Recumbent_bicycle#Steering" title="Recumbent bicycle">steering</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brakes">Brakes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_brake" title="Bicycle brake">Bicycle brake</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brake.agr-edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1076" data-file-width="938" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Brake.agr-edit.jpg/170px-Brake.agr-edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Brake.agr-edit.jpg/255px-Brake.agr-edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Brake.agr-edit.jpg/340px-Brake.agr-edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brake.agr-edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Linear-pull brake, also known by the <a href="/wiki/Shimano" title="Shimano">Shimano</a> trademark: V-Brake, on rear wheel of a <a href="/wiki/Mountain_bike" title="Mountain bike">mountain bike</a></div></div></div>
<p>Bicycle brakes may be rim brakes, in which friction pads are compressed against the wheel rims; hub brakes, where the mechanism is contained within the wheel hub, or disc brakes, where pads act on a rotor attached to the hub. Most road bicycles use rim brakes, but some use disk brakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Disc_brakes" title="Disc brakes">Disc brakes</a> are more common for mountain bikes, tandems and recumbent bicycles than on other types of bicycles, due to their increased power, coupled with an increased weight and complexity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BrakeDiskVR.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/BrakeDiskVR.JPG/220px-BrakeDiskVR.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/BrakeDiskVR.JPG/330px-BrakeDiskVR.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/BrakeDiskVR.JPG/440px-BrakeDiskVR.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BrakeDiskVR.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A front disc brake, mounted to the <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_fork" title="Bicycle fork">fork</a> and hub</div></div></div>
<p>With hand-operated brakes, force is applied to brake levers mounted on the handlebars and transmitted via Bowden cables or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> lines to the friction pads, which apply pressure to the braking surface, causing friction which slows the bicycle down. A rear hub brake may be either hand-operated or pedal-actuated, as in the back pedal <i>coaster brakes</i> which were popular in North America until the 1960s.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Track_bicycle" title="Track bicycle">Track bicycles</a> do not have brakes, because all riders ride in the same direction around a track which does not necessitate sharp deceleration. Track riders are still able to slow down because all track bicycles are fixed-gear, meaning that there is no freewheel. Without a freewheel, coasting is impossible, so when the rear wheel is moving, the cranks are moving. To slow down, the rider applies resistance to the pedals, acting as a braking system which can be as effective as a conventional rear wheel brake, but not as effective as a front wheel brake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Suspension">Suspension</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_suspension" title="Bicycle suspension">Bicycle suspension</a></div>
<p>Bicycle suspension refers to the system or systems used to <i>suspend</i> the rider and all or part of the bicycle. This serves two purposes: to keep the wheels in continuous contact with the ground, improving control, and to isolate the rider and luggage from jarring due to rough surfaces, improving comfort.
</p><p>Bicycle suspensions are used primarily on mountain bicycles, but are also common on hybrid bicycles, as they can help deal with problematic vibration from poor surfaces. Suspension is especially important on recumbent bicycles, since while an upright bicycle rider can stand on the pedals to achieve some of the benefits of suspension, a recumbent rider cannot.
</p><p>Basic mountain bicycles and hybrids usually have front suspension only, whilst more sophisticated ones also have rear suspension. Road bicycles tend to have no suspension.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wheels_and_tires">Wheels and tires</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_wheel" title="Bicycle wheel">Bicycle wheel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_tire" title="Bicycle tire">Bicycle tire</a></div>
<p>The wheel axle fits into fork ends in the frame and fork. A pair of wheels may be called a wheelset, especially in the context of ready-built "off the shelf", performance-oriented wheels.
</p><p>Tires vary enormously depending on their intended purpose. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Road_bicycles" title="Road bicycles">Road bicycles</a> use tires 18 to 25 millimeters wide, most often completely smooth, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slick_tire" title="Slick tire">slick</a>, and inflated to high pressure to roll fast on smooth surfaces. Off-road tires are usually between 38 and 64 mm (1.5 and 2.5 in) wide, and have treads for gripping in muddy conditions or metal studs for ice.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Groupset">Groupset</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Groupset" title="Groupset">Groupset</a> generally refers to all of the components that make up a bicycle excluding the bicycle frame, fork, stem, wheels, tires, and rider contact points, such as the saddle and handlebars.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accessories">Accessories</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Loaded_touring_bicycle.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2489" data-file-width="3320" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Loaded_touring_bicycle.JPG/220px-Loaded_touring_bicycle.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Loaded_touring_bicycle.JPG/330px-Loaded_touring_bicycle.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Loaded_touring_bicycle.JPG/440px-Loaded_touring_bicycle.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Loaded_touring_bicycle.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Touring bicycle equipped with front and rear <a href="/wiki/Luggage_carrier" title="Luggage carrier">racks</a>, fenders (called mud-guards), water bottles in <a href="/wiki/Bottle_cage" title="Bottle cage">cages</a>, four <a href="/wiki/Pannier" title="Pannier">panniers</a> and a handlebar bag</div></div></div>
<p>Some components, which are often optional accessories on sports bicycles, are standard features on utility bicycles to enhance their usefulness, comfort, safety and visibility. <a href="/wiki/Fender_(vehicle)" title="Fender (vehicle)">Fenders</a> with spoilers (mudflaps) protect the cyclist and moving parts from spray when riding through wet areas. In some countries (e.g. Germany, UK), fenders are called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mudguard" title="Mudguard">mudguards</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chainguard" title="Chainguard">chainguards</a> protect clothes from oil on the chain while preventing clothing from being caught between the chain and <a href="/wiki/Crankset" title="Crankset">crankset</a> teeth. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kick_stand" title="Kick stand">Kick stands</a> keep bicycles upright when parked, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bike_lock" title="Bike lock">bike locks</a> deter theft. Front-mounted <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_basket" title="Bicycle basket">baskets</a>, front or rear <a href="/wiki/Luggage_carrier" title="Luggage carrier">luggage carriers</a> or racks, and <a href="/wiki/Pannier" title="Pannier">panniers</a> mounted above either or both wheels can be used to carry equipment or cargo. Pegs can be fastened to one, or both of the wheel hubs to either help the rider perform certain tricks, or allow a place for extra riders to stand, or rest.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Parents sometimes add rear-mounted <a href="/wiki/Baby_transport" title="Baby transport">child seats</a>, an auxiliary saddle fitted to the crossbar, or both to transport children. Bicycles can also be fitted with a hitch to tow a <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_trailer" title="Bicycle trailer">trailer</a> for carrying cargo, a child, or both.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clipless#Clipless_pedals" title="Clipless">Toe-clips</a> and toestraps and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clipless_pedals" title="Clipless pedals">clipless pedals</a> help keep the foot locked in the proper pedal position and enable cyclists to pull and push the pedals. Technical accessories include <a href="/wiki/Cyclocomputer" title="Cyclocomputer">cyclocomputers</a> for measuring speed, distance, heart rate, GPS data etc. Other accessories include <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_lighting" title="Bicycle lighting">lights</a>, reflectors, mirrors, racks, trailers, bags, water bottles and <a href="/wiki/Bottle_cage" title="Bottle cage">cages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_bell" title="Bicycle bell">bell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bicycleuniverse_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-bicycleuniverse-60">[60]</a></sup> Bicycle lights, reflectors, and helmets are required by law in some geographic regions depending on the legal code. It is more common to see bicycles with bottle generators, dynamos, lights, fenders, racks and bells in Europe. Bicyclists also have specialized form fitting and high visibility clothing.
</p><p>Children's bicycles may be outfitted with cosmetic enhancements such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicycle_horn" title="Bicycle horn">bike horns</a>, streamers, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spokey_Dokes" title="Spokey Dokes">spoke beads</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Training_wheels" title="Training wheels">Training wheels</a> are sometimes used when learning to ride.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bicycle_helmet" title="Bicycle helmet">Bicycle helmets</a> can reduce injury in the event of a collision or accident, and a suitable helmet is legally required of riders in many jurisdictions. Helmets may be classified as an accessory<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bicycleuniverse_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-bicycleuniverse-60">[60]</a></sup> or as an item of clothing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Bicycle_trainer" title="Bicycle trainer">Bike trainers</a> are used to enable cyclists to cycle while the bike remains stationary. They are frequently used to warm up before races or indoors when riding conditions are unfavorable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standards">Standards</span></h3>
<p>A number of formal and industry standards exist for bicycle components to help make spare parts exchangeable and to maintain a minimum product safety.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">International Organization for Standardization</a> (ISO) has a special technical committee for cycles, TC149, that has the scope of "Standardization in the field of cycles, their components and accessories with particular reference to terminology, testing methods and requirements for performance and safety, and interchangeability".
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Committee_for_Standardization" title="European Committee for Standardization">European Committee for Standardization</a> (CEN) also has a specific Technical Committee, TC333, that defines European standards for cycles. Their mandate states that EN cycle standards shall harmonize with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_ISO_standards" title="List of ISO standards">ISO standards</a>. Some CEN cycle standards were developed before ISO published their standards, leading to strong European influences in this area. European cycle standards tend to describe minimum safety requirements, while ISO standards have historically harmonized parts geometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[note 1]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Maintenance_and_repair">Maintenance and repair</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bicycle&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Like all devices with mechanical moving parts, bicycles require a certain amount of regular maintenance and replacement of worn parts. A bicycle is relatively simple compared with a car, so some cyclists choose to do at least part of the maintenance themselves. Some components are easy to handle using relatively simple tools, while other components may require specialist manufacturer-dependent tools.
</p><p>Many bicycle components are available at several different price/quality points; manufacturers generally try to keep all components on any particular bike at about the same quality level, though at the very cheap end of the market there may be some skimping on less obvious components (e.g. bottom bracket).
</p>
<ul><li>There are several hundred assisted-service Community Bicycle Organizations worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> At a Community Bicycle Organization, <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/layperson#Noun" title="wikt:layperson">laypeople</a> bring in bicycles needing repair or maintenance; volunteers teach them how to do the required steps.</li>
<li>Full service is available from <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_mechanic" title="Bicycle mechanic">bicycle mechanics</a> at a <a href="/wiki/Local_bike_shop" title="Local bike shop">local bike shop</a>.</li>
<li>In areas where it is available, some cyclists purchase roadside assistance from companies such as the <a href="/wiki/Better_World_Club" title="Better World Club">Better World Club</a> or the <a href="/wiki/American_Automobile_Association" title="American Automobile Association">American Automobile Association</a>.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maintenance">Maintenance</span></h3>
<p>The most basic maintenance item is keeping the tires correctly inflated; this can make a noticeable difference as to how the bike feels to ride. Bicycle tires usually have a marking on the sidewall indicating the pressure appropriate for that tire. Bicycles use much higher pressures than cars: car tires are normally in the range of 30 to 40 pounds per square inch (210 to 280 kPa), whereas bicycle tires are normally in the range of 60 to 100 pounds per square inch (410 to 690 kPa).
</p><p>Another basic maintenance item is regular lubrication of the chain and pivot points for derailleurs and brake components. Most of the bearings on a modern bike are sealed and grease-filled and require little or no attention; such bearings will usually last for 10,000 miles (16,000 km) or more. The crank bearings require periodic maintenance, which involves removing, cleaning and repacking with the correct grease.
</p><p>The chain and the brake blocks are the components which wear out most quickly, so these need to be checked from time to time, typically every 500 miles (800 km) or so. Most local bike shops will do such checks for free. Note that when a chain becomes badly worn it will also wear out the rear cogs/cassette and eventually the chain ring(s), so replacing a chain when only moderately worn will prolong the life of other components.
</p><p>Over the longer term, tires do wear out, after 2,000 to 5,000 miles (3,200 to 8,000 km); a rash of punctures is often the most visible sign of a worn tire.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Repair">Repair</span></h3>
<p>Very few bicycle components can actually be repaired; replacement of the failing component is the normal practice.
</p><p>The most common roadside problem is a puncture. After removing the offending nail/tack/thorn/glass shard/etc., there are two approaches: either mend the puncture by the roadside, or replace the inner tube and then mend the puncture in the comfort of home. Some brands of tires are much more <a href="/wiki/Puncture_resistance" title="Puncture resistance">puncture-resistant</a> than others, often incorporating one or more layers of <a href="/wiki/Kevlar" title="Kevlar">Kevlar</a>; the downside of such tires is that they may be heavier and/or more difficult to fit and remove.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tools">Tools</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_tools" title="Bicycle tools">Bicycle tools</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puncture-repaire-kit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1350" data-file-width="1902" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Puncture-repaire-kit.jpg/220px-Puncture-repaire-kit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Puncture-repaire-kit.jpg/330px-Puncture-repaire-kit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Puncture-repaire-kit.jpg/440px-Puncture-repaire-kit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puncture-repaire-kit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Puncture repair kit with tire levers, <a href="/wiki/Sandpaper" title="Sandpaper">sandpaper</a> to clean off an area of the inner tube around the puncture, a tube of rubber solution (<a href="/wiki/Vulcanization" title="Vulcanization">vulcanizing</a> fluid), round and oval patches, a metal grater and piece of chalk to make chalk powder (to dust over excess rubber solution). Kits often also include a wax crayon to mark the puncture location.</div></div></div>
<p>There are specialized bicycle tools for use both in the shop and at the roadside. Many cyclists carry tool kits. These may include a tire patch kit (which, in turn, may contain any combination of a <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_pump" title="Bicycle pump">hand pump</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_pump#CO2_Inflators" title="Bicycle pump">CO<sub>2</sub> pump</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tire_lever" title="Tire lever">tire levers</a>, spare <a href="/wiki/Inner_tube" title="Inner tube">tubes</a>, self-adhesive patches, or tube-patching material, an adhesive, a piece of sandpaper or a metal grater (for roughing the tube surface to be patched) and sometimes even a block of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/French_chalk" title="French chalk">French chalk</a>), <a href="/wiki/Wrench" title="Wrench">wrenches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hex_key" title="Hex key">hex keys</a>, screwdrivers, and a <a href="/wiki/Chain_tool" title="Chain tool">chain tool</a>. Special, thin wrenches are often required for maintaining various screw-fastened parts, specifically, the frequently lubricated ball-bearing "cones".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> There are also cycling-specific <a href="/wiki/Multi-tool" title="Multi-tool">multi-tools</a> that combine many of these implements into a single compact device. More specialized bicycle components may require more complex tools, including proprietary tools specific for a given manufacturer.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_and_historical_aspects">Social and historical aspects</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bicycle&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The bicycle has had a considerable effect on human society, in both the cultural and industrial realms.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_daily_life">In daily life</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cycling_infrastructure" title="Cycling infrastructure">Cycling infrastructure</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_cycling_infrastructure" title="History of cycling infrastructure">History of cycling infrastructure</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyclists_in_a_Greymouth_street,_passing_the_premises_of_Mrs_S_Beresford,_dressmaker,_between_1898_and_1905_(4641058720).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2510" data-file-width="3338" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cyclists_in_a_Greymouth_street%2C_passing_the_premises_of_Mrs_S_Beresford%2C_dressmaker%2C_between_1898_and_1905_%284641058720%29.jpg/220px-Cyclists_in_a_Greymouth_street%2C_passing_the_premises_of_Mrs_S_Beresford%2C_dressmaker%2C_between_1898_and_1905_%284641058720%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cyclists_in_a_Greymouth_street%2C_passing_the_premises_of_Mrs_S_Beresford%2C_dressmaker%2C_between_1898_and_1905_%284641058720%29.jpg/330px-Cyclists_in_a_Greymouth_street%2C_passing_the_premises_of_Mrs_S_Beresford%2C_dressmaker%2C_between_1898_and_1905_%284641058720%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cyclists_in_a_Greymouth_street%2C_passing_the_premises_of_Mrs_S_Beresford%2C_dressmaker%2C_between_1898_and_1905_%284641058720%29.jpg/440px-Cyclists_in_a_Greymouth_street%2C_passing_the_premises_of_Mrs_S_Beresford%2C_dressmaker%2C_between_1898_and_1905_%284641058720%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyclists_in_a_Greymouth_street,_passing_the_premises_of_Mrs_S_Beresford,_dressmaker,_between_1898_and_1905_(4641058720).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cyclists in <a href="/wiki/Greymouth" title="Greymouth">Greymouth</a>, New Zealand (c.1898-1905)</div></div></div>
<p>Around the turn of the 20th century, bicycles reduced crowding in inner-city tenements by allowing workers to commute from more spacious dwellings in the suburbs. They also reduced dependence on horses. Bicycles allowed people to travel for leisure into the country, since bicycles were three times as energy efficient as walking and three to four times as fast.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Canal_Forsyth_Streets_bikeway_jeh.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1928" data-file-width="1996" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Canal_Forsyth_Streets_bikeway_jeh.JPG/220px-Canal_Forsyth_Streets_bikeway_jeh.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Canal_Forsyth_Streets_bikeway_jeh.JPG/330px-Canal_Forsyth_Streets_bikeway_jeh.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Canal_Forsyth_Streets_bikeway_jeh.JPG/440px-Canal_Forsyth_Streets_bikeway_jeh.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Canal_Forsyth_Streets_bikeway_jeh.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bikeway in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, USA (2008)</div></div></div>
<p>In built-up cities around the world, <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> uses <a href="/wiki/Cycling_infrastructure" title="Cycling infrastructure">cycling infrastructure</a> like bikeways to reduce <a href="/wiki/Traffic_congestion" title="Traffic congestion">traffic congestion</a> and air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> A number of cities around the world have implemented schemes known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicycle_sharing_system" title="Bicycle sharing system">bicycle sharing systems</a> or community bicycle programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> The first of these was the White Bicycle plan in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a> in 1965. It was followed by yellow bicycles in <a href="/wiki/La_Rochelle" title="La Rochelle">La Rochelle</a> and green bicycles in Cambridge. These initiatives complement public transport systems and offer an alternative to motorized traffic to help reduce congestion and pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> In Europe, especially in the Netherlands and parts of Germany and Denmark, bicycle commuting is common. In Copenhagen, a cyclists' organization runs a Cycling Embassy that promotes biking for commuting and sightseeing. The United Kingdom has a tax break scheme (IR 176) that allows employees to buy a new bicycle tax free to use for commuting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a> all train stations offer free <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_parking" title="Bicycle parking">bicycle parking</a>, or a more secure parking place for a small fee, with the larger stations also offering bicycle repair shops. Cycling is so popular that the parking capacity may be exceeded, while in some places such as Delft the capacity is usually exceeded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a> in Norway, the <a href="/wiki/Trampe_bicycle_lift" title="Trampe bicycle lift">Trampe bicycle lift</a> has been developed to encourage cyclists by giving assistance on a steep hill. Buses in many cities have <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_carrier" title="Bicycle carrier">bicycle carriers</a> mounted on the front.
</p><p>There are towns in some countries where <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_culture" title="Bicycle culture">bicycle culture</a> has been an integral part of the landscape for generations, even without much official support. That is the case of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%8Dlhavo_Municipality" title="Ílhavo Municipality">Ílhavo</a>, in Portugal.
</p><p>In cities where bicycles are not integrated into the public transportation system, commuters often use bicycles as elements of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mixed-mode_commuting" title="Mixed-mode commuting">mixed-mode commute</a>, where the bike is used to travel to and from train stations or other forms of rapid transit. Some students who commute several miles drive a car from home to a campus parking lot, then ride a bicycle to class. <a href="/wiki/Folding_bicycle" title="Folding bicycle">Folding bicycles</a> are useful in these scenarios, as they are less cumbersome when carried aboard. Los Angeles removed a small amount of seating on some trains to make more room for bicycles and wheel chairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyclists_at_red_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1777" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cyclists_at_red_2.jpg/220px-Cyclists_at_red_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cyclists_at_red_2.jpg/330px-Cyclists_at_red_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Cyclists_at_red_2.jpg/440px-Cyclists_at_red_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyclists_at_red_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Urban cyclists in <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>, Denmark, at a traffic light</div></div></div>
<p>Some US companies, notably in the <a href="/wiki/High_tech#Technology_sectors" title="High tech">tech sector</a>, are developing both innovative cycle designs and cycle-friendliness in the workplace. <a href="/wiki/Foursquare_(company)" title="Foursquare (company)">Foursquare</a>, whose CEO <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Crowley" title="Dennis Crowley">Dennis Crowley</a> "pedaled to pitch meetings ... [when he] was raising money from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venture_capitalists" title="Venture capitalists">venture capitalists</a>" on a two-wheeler, chose a new location for its New York headquarters "based on where biking would be easy". Parking in the office was also integral to HQ planning. Mitchell Moss, who runs the <a href="/wiki/Rudin_Center_for_Transportation_Policy_%26_Management" title="Rudin Center for Transportation Policy &amp; Management">Rudin Center for Transportation Policy &amp; Management</a> at <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">New York University</a>, said in 2012: "Biking has become the mode of choice for the educated high tech worker".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bicycles offer an important mode of transport in many developing countries. Until recently, bicycles have been a staple of everyday life throughout Asian countries. They are the most frequently used method of transport for commuting to work, school, shopping, and life in general. In Europe, bicycles are commonly used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> They also offer a degree of exercise to keep individuals healthy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bicycles are also celebrated in the visual arts. An example of this is the <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_Film_Festival" title="Bicycle Film Festival">Bicycle Film Festival</a>, a film festival hosted all around the world.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty_alleviation">Poverty alleviation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Banana-bike.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2974" data-file-width="4461" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Banana-bike.jpg/220px-Banana-bike.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Banana-bike.jpg/330px-Banana-bike.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Banana-bike.jpg/440px-Banana-bike.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Banana-bike.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Men in Uganda using a bicycle to transport bananas</div></div></div><div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_poverty_reduction" title="Bicycle poverty reduction">Bicycle poverty reduction</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bicycle_poverty_reduction&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bicycle_transport_of_Hay_edit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1067" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bicycle_transport_of_Hay_edit.jpg/220px-Bicycle_transport_of_Hay_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bicycle_transport_of_Hay_edit.jpg/330px-Bicycle_transport_of_Hay_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Bicycle_transport_of_Hay_edit.jpg/440px-Bicycle_transport_of_Hay_edit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bicycle_transport_of_Hay_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tanzanian boy transporting fodder on his bicycle to feed his family cattle</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bicycle_poverty_reduction" title="Bicycle poverty reduction">Bicycle poverty reduction</a> is the concept that access to bicycles and the transportation infrastructure to support them can dramatically <a href="/wiki/Poverty_reduction" title="Poverty reduction">reduce poverty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> This has been demonstrated in various pilot projects in South Asia and Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bicycle_poverty_reduction_BicyclePotential_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bicycle_poverty_reduction_BicyclePotential-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Experiments done in Africa (<a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> on hundreds of households have shown that a bicycle can increase the income of a poor family by as much as 35%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bicycle_poverty_reduction_BicyclePotential_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bicycle_poverty_reduction_BicyclePotential-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
Transport, if analyzed for the cost–benefit analysis for <a href="/wiki/Rural_poverty" title="Rural poverty">rural poverty</a> alleviation, has given one of the best returns in this regard. For example, road investments in India were a staggering 3–10 times more effective than almost all other investments and subsidies in <a href="/wiki/Rural_economics" title="Rural economics">rural economy</a> in the decade of the 1990s. A road can ease transport on a macro level, while bicycle access supports it at the micro level. In that sense, the bicycle can be one of the most effective means to eradicate poverty in poor nations.</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Female_emancipation">Female emancipation</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bicycling_and_feminism" title="Bicycling and feminism">Bicycling and feminism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_wheel_within_a_wheel_page_56.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="878" data-file-width="657" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/A_wheel_within_a_wheel_page_56.jpg/170px-A_wheel_within_a_wheel_page_56.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/A_wheel_within_a_wheel_page_56.jpg/255px-A_wheel_within_a_wheel_page_56.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/A_wheel_within_a_wheel_page_56.jpg/340px-A_wheel_within_a_wheel_page_56.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_wheel_within_a_wheel_page_56.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Let go – but stand by"; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frances_Willard_(suffragist)" title="Frances Willard (suffragist)">Frances Willard</a> learning to ride a bicycle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willard1895_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Willard1895-87">[86]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The safety bicycle gave women unprecedented mobility, contributing to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emancipation_of_women" title="Emancipation of women">their emancipation</a> in Western nations. As bicycles became safer and cheaper, more women had access to the personal freedom that bicycles embodied, and so the bicycle came to symbolize the <a href="/wiki/New_Woman" title="New Woman">New Woman</a> of the late 19th century, especially in Britain and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004266–71_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004266–71-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Distillations_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Distillations-88">[87]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bike_boom#1890s" title="Bike boom">bicycle craze in the 1890s</a> also led to a movement for so-called <a href="/wiki/Victorian_dress_reform" title="Victorian dress reform">rational dress</a>, which helped liberate women from corsets and ankle-length skirts and other restrictive garments, substituting the then-shocking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloomers_(clothing)" title="Bloomers (clothing)">bloomers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004266–71_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerlihy2004266–71-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>The bicycle was recognized by 19th-century feminists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suffragist" title="Suffragist">suffragists</a> as a "freedom machine" for women. American <a href="/wiki/Susan_B._Anthony" title="Susan B. Anthony">Susan B. Anthony</a> said in a <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_World" title="New York World">New York World</a></i> interview on 2 February 1896: "I think it has done more to emancipate woman than any one thing in the world. I rejoice every time I see a woman ride by on a wheel. It gives her a feeling of self-reliance and independence the moment she takes her seat; and away she goes, the picture of untrammelled womanhood."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HustedHarper1898_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-HustedHarper1898-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 859">: 859 </span></sup> In 1895 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frances_Willard_(suffragist)" title="Frances Willard (suffragist)">Frances Willard</a>, the tightly laced president of the <a href="/wiki/Woman%27s_Christian_Temperance_Union" title="Woman's Christian Temperance Union">Woman's Christian Temperance Union</a>, wrote <i>A Wheel Within a Wheel: How I Learned to Ride the Bicycle, with Some Reflections by the Way</i>, a 75-page illustrated memoir praising "Gladys", her bicycle, for its "gladdening effect" on her health and political optimism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willard1895_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Willard1895-87">[86]</a></sup> Willard used a cycling metaphor to urge other suffragists to action.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Willard1895_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Willard1895-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1985, Georgena Terry started the first women-specific bicycle company. Her designs featured frame geometry and wheel sizes chosen to better fit women, with shorter top tubes and more suitable reach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_implications">Economic implications</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Columbia_Bicycles_1886_Advertisement.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="453" data-file-width="317" decoding="async" height="243" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Columbia_Bicycles_1886_Advertisement.svg/170px-Columbia_Bicycles_1886_Advertisement.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Columbia_Bicycles_1886_Advertisement.svg/255px-Columbia_Bicycles_1886_Advertisement.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Columbia_Bicycles_1886_Advertisement.svg/340px-Columbia_Bicycles_1886_Advertisement.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Columbia_Bicycles_1886_Advertisement.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbia_Bicycles" title="Columbia Bicycles">Columbia Bicycles</a> advertisement from 1886</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bicycle_industry" title="Bicycle industry">Bicycle manufacturing</a> proved to be a training ground for other industries and led to the development of advanced metalworking techniques, both for the frames themselves and for special components such as <a href="/wiki/Ball_bearing" title="Ball bearing">ball bearings</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washer_(mechanical)" title="Washer (mechanical)">washers</a>, and sprockets. These techniques later enabled skilled metalworkers and mechanics to develop the components used in early automobiles and aircraft.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers" title="Wright brothers">Wilbur and Orville Wright</a>, a pair of businessmen, ran the <a href="/wiki/Wright_Cycle_Company" title="Wright Cycle Company">Wright Cycle Company</a> which designed, manufactured and sold their bicycles during the <a href="/wiki/Bike_boom" title="Bike boom">bike boom</a> of the 1890s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WDL_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-WDL-91">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>They also served to teach the industrial models later adopted, including mechanization and <a href="/wiki/Mass_production" title="Mass production">mass production</a> (later copied and adopted by <a href="/wiki/Ford_Motor_Company" title="Ford Motor Company">Ford</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Motors" title="General Motors">General Motors</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200123_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200123-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001106_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001106-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001108_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001108-94">[93]</a></sup> vertical integration<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001106_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001106-93">[92]</a></sup> (also later copied and adopted by Ford), aggressive advertising<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001142–47_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001142–47-95">[94]</a></sup> (as much as 10% of all advertising in U.S. periodicals in 1898 was by bicycle makers),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001145_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001145-96">[95]</a></sup> lobbying for better roads (which had the side benefit of acting as advertising, and of improving sales by providing more places to ride),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001108_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001108-94">[93]</a></sup> all first practiced by Pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001108_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001108-94">[93]</a></sup> In addition, bicycle makers adopted the annual model change<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200123_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200123-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> (later derided as <a href="/wiki/Planned_obsolescence" title="Planned obsolescence">planned obsolescence</a>, and usually credited to General Motors), which proved very successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early bicycles were an example of <a href="/wiki/Conspicuous_consumption" title="Conspicuous consumption">conspicuous consumption</a>, being adopted by the fashionable elites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe20018_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe20018-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200112_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200112-100">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200114_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200114-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200123_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200123-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001147–48_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001147–48-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001187–88_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001187–88-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001208_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001208-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001243–45_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001243–45-105">[104]</a></sup> In addition, by serving as a platform for accessories, which could ultimately cost more than the bicycle itself, it paved the way for the likes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barbie_doll" title="Barbie doll">Barbie doll</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200123_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200123-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001121_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001121-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001123_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001123-107">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bicycles helped create, or enhance, new kinds of businesses, such as bicycle messengers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001212_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001212-108">[107]</a></sup> traveling seamstresses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001214_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001214-109">[108]</a></sup> riding academies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001131_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001131-110">[109]</a></sup> and racing rinks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200130_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe200130-111">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001131_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001131-110">[109]</a></sup> Their board tracks were later adapted to early <a href="/wiki/Motorcycle_racing" title="Motorcycle racing">motorcycle</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile_racing" title="Automobile racing">automobile racing</a>. There were a variety of new inventions, such as <a href="/wiki/Spoke" title="Spoke">spoke</a> tighteners,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001125_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001125-112">[111]</a></sup> and specialized lights,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001123_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001123-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001125_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001125-112">[111]</a></sup> socks and shoes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001125–26_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001125–26-113">[112]</a></sup> and even cameras, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastman_Kodak" title="Eastman Kodak">Eastman Company</a>'s Poco.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001238_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001238-114">[113]</a></sup> Probably the best known and most widely used of these inventions, adopted well beyond cycling, is Charles Bennett's Bike Web, which came to be called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jock_strap" title="Jock strap">jock strap</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001128_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001128-115">[114]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Person_mit_fahrrad_feb07.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Person_mit_fahrrad_feb07.jpg/170px-Person_mit_fahrrad_feb07.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Person_mit_fahrrad_feb07.jpg/255px-Person_mit_fahrrad_feb07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Person_mit_fahrrad_feb07.jpg/340px-Person_mit_fahrrad_feb07.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Person_mit_fahrrad_feb07.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A man uses a bicycle to carry goods in <a href="/wiki/Ouagadougou" title="Ouagadougou">Ouagadougou</a>, Burkina Faso.</div></div></div>
<p>They also presaged a move away from public transit<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001214–15_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001214–15-116">[115]</a></sup> that would explode with the introduction of the automobile.
</p><p>J. K. Starley's company became the Rover Cycle Company Ltd. in the late 1890s, and then simply the <a href="/wiki/Rover_Company" title="Rover Company">Rover Company</a> when it started making cars. <a href="/wiki/Morris_Motors" title="Morris Motors">Morris Motors</a> Limited (in <a href="/wiki/Oxford" title="Oxford">Oxford</a>) and <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0koda_Auto" title="Škoda Auto">Škoda</a> also began in the bicycle business, as did the <a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers" title="Wright brothers">Wright brothers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup> Alistair Craig, whose company eventually emerged to become the engine manufacturers <a href="/wiki/Ailsa_Craig_Engines" title="Ailsa Craig Engines">Ailsa Craig</a>, also started from manufacturing bicycles, in Glasgow in March 1885.
</p><p>In general, U.S. and European cycle manufacturers used to assemble cycles from their own frames and components made by other companies, although very large companies (such as Raleigh) used to make almost every part of a bicycle (including bottom brackets, axles, etc.) In recent years, those bicycle makers have greatly changed their methods of production. Now, almost none of them produce their own frames.
</p><p>Many newer or smaller companies only design and market their products; the actual production is done by Asian companies. For example, some 60% of the world's bicycles are now being made in China. Despite this shift in production, as nations such as China and India become more wealthy, their own use of bicycles has declined due to the increasing affordability of cars and motorcycles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> One of the major reasons for the proliferation of Chinese-made bicycles in foreign markets is the lower cost of labor in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p><p>In line with the European financial crisis, in Italy in 2011 the number of bicycle sales (1.75 million) just passed the number of new car sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Utrecht_-_panoramio_(2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Utrecht_-_panoramio_%282%29.jpg/220px-Utrecht_-_panoramio_%282%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Utrecht_-_panoramio_%282%29.jpg/330px-Utrecht_-_panoramio_%282%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Utrecht_-_panoramio_%282%29.jpg/440px-Utrecht_-_panoramio_%282%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Utrecht_-_panoramio_(2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bicycles in <a href="/wiki/Utrecht" title="Utrecht">Utrecht</a>, Netherlands</div></div></div>
<p>One of the profound economic implications of bicycle use is that it liberates the user from <a href="/wiki/Motor_fuel" title="Motor fuel">motor fuel</a> consumption. (Ballantine, 1972) The bicycle is an inexpensive, fast, healthy and environmentally friendly mode of transport. <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Illich" title="Ivan Illich">Ivan Illich</a> stated that bicycle use extended the usable physical environment for people, while alternatives such as cars and motorways degraded and confined people's environment and mobility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> Currently, two billion bicycles are in use around the world. Children, students, professionals, laborers, civil servants and seniors are pedaling around their communities. They all experience the freedom and the natural opportunity for exercise that the bicycle easily provides. Bicycle also has lowest carbon intensity of travel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_implications">Religious implications</span></h3>
<p>The proper <a href="/wiki/Islamic_bicycle" title="Islamic bicycle">Islamic bicycle</a> for the Iranian women is a topic of heated discussion in both Sunni and Shia Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing">Manufacturing</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_bicycle_manufacturing_companies" title="List of bicycle manufacturing companies">List of bicycle manufacturing companies</a></div>
<p>The global bicycle market is $61 billion in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> As of 2009<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Bicycle&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> 130 million bicycles were sold every year globally and 66% of them were made in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_requirements">Legal requirements</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_law" title="Bicycle law">Bicycle law</a></div>
<p>Early in its development, as with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automobile" title="Automobile">automobiles</a>, there were restrictions on the operation of bicycles. Along with advertising, and to gain free publicity, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albert_A._Pope" title="Albert A. Pope">Albert A. Pope</a> litigated on behalf of cyclists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001108_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorcliffe2001108-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1968 <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_Road_Traffic" title="Vienna Convention on Road Traffic">Vienna Convention on Road Traffic</a> of the United Nations considers a bicycle to be a vehicle, and a person controlling a bicycle (whether actually riding or not) is considered an operator. The traffic codes of many countries reflect these definitions and demand that a bicycle satisfy certain legal requirements before it can be used on public roads. In many <a href="/wiki/Jurisdiction" title="Jurisdiction">jurisdictions</a>, it is an offense to use a bicycle that is not in a roadworthy condition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In most jurisdictions, bicycles must have functioning front and rear lights when ridden after dark. As some generator or <a href="/wiki/Dynamo" title="Dynamo">dynamo</a>-driven lamps operate only while moving, rear <a href="/wiki/Retroreflector" title="Retroreflector">reflectors</a> are frequently also mandatory. Since a moving bicycle makes little noise, some countries insist that bicycles have a warning bell for use when approaching pedestrians, equestrians, and other cyclists, though sometimes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_horn" title="Car horn">car horn</a> can be used when a 12 volt battery is available.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Some countries require child and/or adult cyclists to wear helmets, as this may protect riders from head trauma. Countries which require adult cyclists to wear helmets include Spain, <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_helmets_in_New_Zealand" title="Bicycle helmets in New Zealand">New Zealand</a> and Australia. Mandatory helmet wearing is one of the most controversial topics in the cycling world, with proponents arguing that it reduces head injuries and thus is an acceptable requirement, while opponents argue that by making cycling seem more dangerous and cumbersome, it reduces cyclist numbers on the streets, creating an overall negative health effect (fewer people cycling for their own health, and the remaining cyclists being more exposed through a reversed <a href="/wiki/Safety_in_numbers" title="Safety in numbers">safety in numbers</a> effect).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theft">Theft</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bicycle_theft" title="Bicycle theft">Bicycle theft</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2008-09-06_Solitary_bicycle_wheel_in_a_bike_rack.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3888" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/2008-09-06_Solitary_bicycle_wheel_in_a_bike_rack.jpg/170px-2008-09-06_Solitary_bicycle_wheel_in_a_bike_rack.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/2008-09-06_Solitary_bicycle_wheel_in_a_bike_rack.jpg/255px-2008-09-06_Solitary_bicycle_wheel_in_a_bike_rack.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/2008-09-06_Solitary_bicycle_wheel_in_a_bike_rack.jpg/340px-2008-09-06_Solitary_bicycle_wheel_in_a_bike_rack.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2008-09-06_Solitary_bicycle_wheel_in_a_bike_rack.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bicycle wheel remains chained in a bike rack after the rest of the bicycle has been stolen at east campus of <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Durham,_North_Carolina" title="Durham, North Carolina">Durham, North Carolina</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Bicycles are popular targets for theft, due to their value and ease of resale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> The number of bicycles stolen annually is difficult to quantify as a large number of crimes are not reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> Around 50% of the participants in the Montreal International Journal of Sustainable Transportation survey were subjected to a bicycle theft in their lifetime as active cyclists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup> Most bicycles have serial numbers that can be recorded to verify identity in case of theft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The TC149 ISO bicycle committee, including the TC149/SC1 ("Cycles and major sub-assemblies") subcommittee, has published the following standards:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
<ul><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_4210&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 4210 (page does not exist)">ISO 4210</a> Cycles – Safety requirements for bicycles</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_6692&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 6692 (page does not exist)">ISO 6692</a> Cycles – Marking of cycle components</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_6695&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 6695 (page does not exist)">ISO 6695</a> Cycles – Pedal axle and crank assembly with square end fitting – Assembly dimensions</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_6696&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 6696 (page does not exist)">ISO 6696</a> Cycles – Screw threads used in bottom bracket assemblies</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_6697&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 6697 (page does not exist)">ISO 6697</a> Cycles – Hubs and freewheels – Assembly dimensions</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_6698&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 6698 (page does not exist)">ISO 6698</a> Cycles – Screw threads used to assemble freewheels on bicycle hubs</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_6699&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 6699 (page does not exist)">ISO 6699</a> Cycles – Stem and handlebar bend – Assembly dimensions</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_6701&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 6701 (page does not exist)">ISO 6701</a> Cycles – External dimensions of spoke nipples</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_6742&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 6742 (page does not exist)">ISO 6742</a> Cycles – Lighting and retro-reflective devices – Photometric and physical requirements</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_8090&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 8090 (page does not exist)">ISO 8090</a> Cycles – Terminology (same as BS 6102-4)</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_8098&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 8098 (page does not exist)">ISO 8098</a> Cycles – Safety requirements for bicycles for young children</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_8488&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 8488 (page does not exist)">ISO 8488</a> Cycles – Screw threads used to assemble head fittings on bicycle forks</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_8562&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 8562 (page does not exist)">ISO 8562</a> Cycles – Stem wedge angle</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_10230&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 10230 (page does not exist)">ISO 10230</a> Cycles – Splined hub and sprocket – Mating dimensions</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_11243&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 11243 (page does not exist)">ISO 11243</a> Cycles – Luggage carriers for bicycles – Concepts, classification and testing</li></ul>

Other ISO Technical Committees have published various cycle relevant standards, for example:
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/ISO_5775" title="ISO 5775">ISO 5775</a> Bicycle tire and rim designations</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=ISO_9633&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="ISO 9633 (page does not exist)">ISO 9633</a> Cycle chains – Characteristics and test methods</li></ul>

Published cycle standards from CEN TC333 include:
<ul><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EN_14764&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EN 14764 (page does not exist)">EN 14764</a> City and trekking bicycles – Safety requirements and test methods</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EN_14765&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EN 14765 (page does not exist)">EN 14765</a> Bicycles for young children – Safety requirements and test methods</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EN_14766&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EN 14766 (page does not exist)">EN 14766</a> Mountain-bicycles – Safety requirements and test methods</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EN_14781&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EN 14781 (page does not exist)">EN 14781</a> Racing bicycles – Safety requirements and test methods</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EN_14782&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EN 14782 (page does not exist)">EN 14782</a> Bicycles – Accessories for bicycles – Luggage carriers</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EN_15496&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EN 15496 (page does not exist)">EN 15496</a> Cycles – Requirements and test methods for cycle locks</li></ul>

Yet to be approved cycle standards from CEN TC333:
<ul><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EN_15194&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EN 15194 (page does not exist)">EN 15194</a> Cycles – Electrically power assisted cycles (<a href="/wiki/Electric_bicycle#CITEREF2015" title="Electric bicycle">EPAC</a> bicycle)</li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=EN_15532&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="EN 15532 (page does not exist)">EN 15532</a> Cycles – Terminology</li>
<li>00333011 Cycles – Bicycles trailers – safety requirements and test methods</li></ul>
</span></li>
</ol></div></div>
